            }
            return "blank";
        }

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e5939466b5ff45a4857b727b455966ab">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '25ba15e9-24c8-4df9-89f0-0d999d005554'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d161322428" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>O3-type cathode material with high theoretical capacity possesses significant potential for sodium ion batteries (SIBs). However, the irreversible phase transition, structural volume change and poor Na<sup>+</sup> transmission efficiency, caused by Jahn–Teller distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup>, lead to the inferior cycling lifespan. Herein, the nonequilibrium-driven local electron spin-state modulation at Mn site with Sn<sup>4+</sup> substitution is proposed to stabilize the NaNi<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode. With this, the controlled irreversible phase transition and volume expansion during charge/discharge and fast Na<sup>+</sup> transportation channel is achieved. Therefore, the modulated NaNi<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode can contribute to improved capacity of 144.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C rate and long-term cycling over 200 cycles with 80.1% retention by comparison with the counterpart (132.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C) and 54.1% retention. Noted that the elevated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics corresponding to high-rate capability is also demonstrated (93.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C rate). Furthermore, the full battery equipped with hard carbon anode shows an energy density of 381.05&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and the 76.8% retention after 200 cycles. This work highlights the regulation of electron spin-state from the insight of modification Jahn–Teller effect would shed a new perception on the design for the advanced layered cathode materials and SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202419967-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202419967-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the growing depletion of limited lithium resources,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0001_R_d161322420e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Rev. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> here is a pressing demand for other energy storage technologies in the upcoming post-LIB era.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0002_R_d161322420e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Nano Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as a viable alternative to reduce dependence on lithium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0003_R_d161322420e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun. Mater. Today">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cathode of SIBs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0004_R_d161322420e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> generally encompass a spectrum of options including Prussian blue analogs (PBAs),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0005_R_d161322420e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> polyanionic compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0006_R_d161322420e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Joule">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> organic<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0007_R_d161322420e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and, layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0008_R_d161322420e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Storage Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, the last one is represented by O3 (Octahedral) and P2 (Prismatio) structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0009_R_d161322420e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Electrochem. Commun. Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Am. Chem. Soc.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Comparatively, the O3-type cathodes boast a higher theoretical capacity than P2-type materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0010_R_d161322420e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics for O3-type cathode (e.g., NaNi<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) becomes an obstacle in the practical use, which associates with the Na<sup>+</sup> transportation channels decided by the interaction of transition metal (e.g., Ni, Mn or Fe) and oxygen (TM─O bonding).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0011_R_d161322420e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nano Energy">11</a>, <a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0012_R_d161322420e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, it is noted that the trivalent Mn (Mn<sup>3+</sup>) has a strong Jahn–Teller distortion, similar to these Mn-containing compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0013_R_d161322420e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Storage Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both of them increase the difficulty of Na<sup>+</sup> mitigation and thereby deteriorate battery capacity and performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0014_R_d161322420e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, how to regulate Mn in the layered O3-type cathode materials is significant for advancing SIBs with high-rate ability.</p>
         
         <p>In recent years, the main strategies known as ion substitution have been adopted to address these above. As reported by Li's group, they used non-chemically active Al<sup>3+</sup> to supersede 40% Mn in the sodium manganate (NaMnO<sub>2</sub>) with the solid-state method and then obtained NaMn<sub>0.6</sub>Al<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0015_R_d161322420e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Small">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such a large replacement leads to the formation of a superlattice in the TM layer, thereby enhancing the structural stability to mitigate Jahn–Teller effect and the reversibility of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox pair. Also, an example that replaces Mn<sup>3+</sup> with Ti<sup>4+</sup> in the O3-type Na<sub>0.9</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials has been explored.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0016_R_d161322420e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Part. Part. Syst. Charact.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The introduction of a multitude Ti<sup>4+</sup> ions would break the charge ordering and Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering due to its larger redox potential difference with Mn<sup>3+</sup>, providing the better Na<sup>+</sup> shuttle in the lattice. Although this, so much ion substitution that decreases the active Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox pair as a whole is the potential to reduce the specific capacity of cathode materials, which belongs to a trade of capacity for structural stability.</p>
         
         <p>By comparison, recommending a few ions for doping at the Mn site could stabilize material structure without the impact of capacity delivery. Sun's group proposed the Mn substitution with 0.05 at. % Li to yield the NaLi<sub>0.05</sub>(Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>0.95</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode materials. After displacement, the structural integrity of the TM layer was improved due to the formation of the stronger Li─O bond. However, such a Li<sup>+</sup> insertion was still difficult to modulate the volume change induced by the Jahn–Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup>, as well as the harmful P'3 phase due to the existence of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox at low potential (≤2.15&nbsp;V, vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0017_R_d161322420e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hu et al utilized multiple metal ions (Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+</sup>, and Sn<sup>4+</sup>) to simultaneously supersede a little of Ni and Mn and then realize a high-entropy configuration (NaNi<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub>). This operation could not only expand the space between the TM layer that facilitates the Na<sup>+</sup> transmission efficiency, but also strengthen the electrochemical stability of this material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0018_R_d161322420e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It can be seen that multi-ion doping can provide improved performance for cathode materials, but people fail to make in-depth perceptions in this aspect, which remains greatly challenging.</p>
         
         <p>In light of classical solid-state chemical theory, the Jahn–Teller effect highly associates with the spin of unpaired electrons at <i>e</i><sub>g</sub> orbital of some transition metals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0019_R_d161322420e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Joule">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inspired by this, in this study, we creatively redesign the O3-type NaNi<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material from the view of local electron valence, which involves the electronic spin-state reconfiguration by the bridging effect of Sn-O-Mn. As shown in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0007">1</a>, in the pristine NaNi<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode (marked as “NNFM”), Mn<sup>3+</sup> shows the high-spin state due to the intrinsic electron distribution of Mn 3d <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/97ec5959-9169-44c4-ac79-15b13a9c14b1/adfm202419967-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202419967:adfm202419967-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202419967-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>t</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <mi>g</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>3</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>e</mi>
                        <mi>g</mi>
                        <mn>1</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$t_{2g}^3e_g^1$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>. After modulation, it is bound to form the configuration of Mn 3d <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/887688b6-4ce6-4cb4-a36c-7b1065ddf472/adfm202419967-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202419967:adfm202419967-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202419967-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>t</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <mi>g</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>3</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>e</mi>
                        <mi>g</mi>
                        <mn>0</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$t_{2g}^3e_g^0$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> with low spin for modified NaNi<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode (marked as “NNFM-S”), which derives from the strong electron-absorbing ability of Sn<sup>4+</sup> toward oxygen atom in Mn-O bond. Such an effect generates a strong dipole polarization field along with the Mn-O-Sn pathway, and acts as an electronic lever, leading to the electron delocalization in the <i>e<sub>g</sub></i> orbital for Mn<sup>3+</sup>, which w effectively relieves the Jahn–Teller distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup>. Additionally, this stable bonding effect of Sn<sup>4+</sup> and O can maintain the structural stability of cathode material, and enlarge the interlayer spacing of TM to achieve rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transportation. Therefore, the half-cell and full battery with NNFM-S cathode expresses a comprehensive and remarkable improvement in capacity, rate performance, and cyclic stability. This work successfully clarifies the regulation of Jahn–Teller effect from the insight of electron spin-state, offering a new perception for redesigning the advanced layered cathode materials and SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202419967-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/12cc4008-2b31-45ed-8ab7-eb4254ede8e3/adfm202419967-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/12cc4008-2b31-45ed-8ab7-eb4254ede8e3/adfm202419967-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/da96e07e-fba1-47f3-8517-1c940de220b5/adfm202419967-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/12cc4008-2b31-45ed-8ab7-eb4254ede8e3/adfm202419967-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         </section>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202419967-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202419967-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurements are employed to reveal the crystal structure of the NaNi<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.33-x</sub>Sn<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (x = 0, 0.01, 0.02, 0.03, 0.04, or 0.05) cathode materials, which were marked as NNFM, NNFM-1S, NNFM-S, NNFM-3S, NNFM-4S, NNFM-5S, respectively (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0001">1a</a>). All diffraction peaks are indexed to a typical O3 structure with the space group of R-3&nbsp;m and without impurity peaks. Notably, the (003) peak of NNFM-S tends to shift to the lower angle with the doped Sn content increasing from 0 to 0.05 at. %, as well as the (104) peak (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0001">1b</a>). This indicates that the Sn<sup>4+</sup> modification can induce the increase of interlayer spacing along with the <i>c</i>-axis direction, and thi effect becomes more obvious as the Sn amount aggrandizes, which is ascribed to the introduction of larger-radius ion into TM layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0020_R_d161322420e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0021_R_d161322420e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Res.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such an introduction leads to the decrease of <i>c</i> parameter while the <i>a</i> and <i>V</i> parameters increase in the crystallographic parameters of NNFM-S sample(Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), which is confirmed by the Rietveld refinement (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S7</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, the as-obtained bonding length of Mn and oxygen (<i>d</i><sub>Mn-O</sub>) for the modified sample expresses a higher value of 1.9357 Å than that in the pristine NNFM (1.9062 Å for Mn─O bond), whereas the Na─O bond (<i>d</i><sub>Na-O</sub>) becomes shortened after Sn<sup>4+</sup> incorporation (2.327 vs 2.373 Å, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0001">1c</a>). Noted that the formed Sn─O bond is also detected with a length of 2.5359 Å, more than the pristine <i>d</i><sub>Mn-O</sub>. These results completely reveal the successful introduction of Sn<sup>4+</sup> into TM layer and their contribution to the expansion of the transition metal layer.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202419967-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6a7c0433-f5ab-4b14-8c5e-7e2c3591240a/adfm202419967-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6a7c0433-f5ab-4b14-8c5e-7e2c3591240a/adfm202419967-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/453de66a-90a7-430b-bd7f-bd77060327c4/adfm202419967-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6a7c0433-f5ab-4b14-8c5e-7e2c3591240a/adfm202419967-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202419967-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202419967" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structure characterizations of NaNi<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.33-X</sub>Sn<sub>X</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. a) XRD patterns and b) enlargement of (003) and (104) peaks. c) Schematic diagram illustrating the crystal structure models shows the layer space and bond distance for NNFM and NNFM-S samples. FESEM images of d) NNFM and e) NNFM-S samples at various magnifications. HRTEM images, the corresponding IFFTs and SAED images of f) NNFM and g) NNFM-S samples.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To detect the morphological changes before and after Sn<sup>4+</sup> modulation, field emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM) was employed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0001">1d,e</a>). Both NNFM and NNFM-S samples possess the platy stacked structure with particle size ranging from 500 to 1500&nbsp;nm. Yet, compared to the agglomerative NNFM, NNFM-S sample shows a good homogeneous dispersibility (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). To further disclose the refined structure of cathode materials before and after modification, we introduced transmission electron microscopy (TEM) to analyze them. It is found that both their patterns belong to the R-3&nbsp;m space group that represents a typical layered structure based on the analysis of selected area electron diffraction (SAED)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0022_R_d161322420e944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0001">1f,g</a> and their insets). This indicates the unchanged layered structure before and after Sn<sup>4+</sup> modification. After amplifying the bulk area, combined with the inverse fast Fourier transformation (IFFT), an obvious interlamellar spacing of 0.501&nbsp;nm corresponding to the plane is detected in the NNFM sample, while the interval NNFM-S sample increases to 0.527&nbsp;nm. Such various results are consistent with XRD Rietveld analysis, demonstrating that Sn<sup>4+</sup> penetration can enlarge the atomic layer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0023_R_d161322420e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Energy Storage Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And the optimal Sn<sup>4+</sup> doping content is controlled at 0.02 at. % due to the formation of rock salt when the introduced Sn is more than 0.03 at. % (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), which is generally known as the interceptor for ion transportation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0024_R_d161322420e968" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, the energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDXS) mapping for surface scan analysis also verifies the uniform distribution of Sn element on the surface and in the interior (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>As depicted in Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0007">1</a>, under the high electrophilicity of Sn<sup>4+</sup>, the bridged Mn-O-Sn pathway can provide the opportunity to delocalize the lone electron in the <i>e<sub>g</sub></i> orbital of Mn<sup>3+</sup>, thereby mitigating the Jahn–Teller effect of revealing this electron delocalization in the <i>e<sub>g</sub></i> orbital, the density functional theory (DFT) calculation was carried out (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0002">2a</a>,<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0002">b</a>). It is obvious that the projected density of states (PDOS) of the <i>e<sub>g</sub></i> orbital occupied for NNFM-S is much lower than that for the pristine NNFM. This confirms the delocalization of electrons in the <i>e<sub>g</sub></i> orbital after Sn<sup>4+</sup> modulation. Besides, the band gap of NNFM sample is evaluated to be as high as 53.42&nbsp;eV at Fermi level, while the NNFM-S delivers a reduced value of 45.32&nbsp;eV in the band gap, indicating the enhanced electronic conductivity after Sn<sup>4+</sup> modulation. Such a modulated effect can be reflected on the Fermi level bandgap of Mn 3<i>d</i> and O 2<i>p</i> orbitals, as well as Sn 3d orbital. The bandgap value of Mn 3<i>d</i> orbital of NNFM-S is evaluated to approach at 0&nbsp;eV, and a similar value is detected in that of O 2<i>p</i> and Sn 3<i>d</i> orbitals (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0002">2d,e</a>). Whereas, the PDOS of Mn 3<i>d</i> and O 2<i>p</i> orbitals in the pristine NNFM provides a bandgap as high as 0.763&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0002">2c</a>). These suggest that the enhancement of the bonding between Mn and O after Sn<sup>4+</sup> modulation, is beneficial to stabilize the lattice oxygen.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202419967-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/73e2ab8c-ab72-4745-8740-a25270479102/adfm202419967-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/73e2ab8c-ab72-4745-8740-a25270479102/adfm202419967-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3900de49-c4d4-4190-88e0-7d0d5148681f/adfm202419967-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/73e2ab8c-ab72-4745-8740-a25270479102/adfm202419967-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202419967-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202419967" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Valence verification. The PDOS and DOS for a) NNFM and b) NNFM-S samples. The PDOS of <i>p</i>, <i>d</i>-orbital for c) NNFM and d) NNFM-S samples, e) the PDOS of O 2<i>p</i>, Mn 3<i>d</i>-orbital for NNFM-S. f) Ex situ Sn 2<i>p</i> XPS spectra of NNFM-S at different states. Ex situ O 1<i>s</i> XPS spectra of g) NNFM and h) NNFM-S at different states. i) Ex situ Mn 2<i>p</i> XPS spectra of NNFM-S at different states.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the difference in electron spin state before and after Sn<sup>4+</sup> modulation, the refined X-ray photoelectrochemical spectroscopy (XPS) was applied to detect their changes in valences with the shifty charge/discharge state. For Sn 3<i>d</i> spectra, only one peak at 498.47&nbsp;eV belonging to Sn 3<i>d</i> is detected at the either charge or discharge state in the NNFM-S sample, indicating that Sn can only support the material structure during the charging and discharging process with no redox activity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0002">2f</a>). Note that this inactive Sn<sup>4+</sup> will induce the valence change of the adjacent oxygen atoms due to the intrinsic electrostatic interaction. As shown in the O 1<i>s</i> spectra, three typical bands at 530.78, 532.92, and 536.29&nbsp;eV appear in the NNFM sample at open circuit voltage (OCV) state, which is ascribed to the lattice oxygen, surface oxygen, and Na KLL peaks, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0002">2g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0025_R_d161322420e1099" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Without regulation, the peak of this lattice oxygen remains almost unchanged in location, yet increases in intensity and area in the whole charging/discharging process. Under comparison, the NNFM-S sample presents the lower peak attributing to lattice oxygen, located at 532.08&nbsp;eV at open circuit voltage (OCV) state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0002">2h</a>), while another two peaks symbolizing surface and Na KLL at 534.18 and 537.98&nbsp;eV are nearly like to that in NNFM. Thereinto, the peak corresponding to lattice oxygen gradually shifts to the lower area as the potential is increased from OCV to the fully charged state (4.0&nbsp;V), indicative of the electron transfer from TM atom to lattice oxygen, which is caused by the induction of high-electro-positivity Sn<sup>4+</sup>. This feature can be sustained through the full state of discharge (2.0&nbsp;V). These also reveal that Sn modulation can drive the local electron valence of the lattice oxygen, and then regulate the electron at the adjacent TM atom.</p>
         
         <p>To know clearly this effect on the definite TM atoms, the Mn 2<i>p</i>, Ni 2<i>p</i>, and Fe 2<i>p</i> spectrums were analyzed carefully to disclose their valences. As shown in Mn 2<i>p</i> spectra, the two typical peaks corresponding to Mn 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> and Mn 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> (654.66 and 641.76&nbsp;eV) were observed in both NNFM and NNFM-S samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), respectively. After subtle fitting, the peaks are convolved to correspond to the two valence states of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0026_R_d161322420e1134" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Solid State Chem. J. Alloys Compd.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The former as the precipitating factor for Jahn–Teller effect can lead to the migration and dissolution of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and structural collapse of layered oxide cathode materials. It is Obviously seen that the Mn<sup>3+</sup>-content becomes diminished to form Mn<sup>4+</sup> after modulation, reflected by the reduced peak area. Further, ex-situ XPS were utilized to investigate this inhibition mechanism of Jahn–Teller effect. In the NNFM sample, Mn 2<i>p</i> spectrum convolves into three pairs of peaks indexing Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>3+</sup>, and Mn<sup>4+</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). With the proceeding of charging and charging, the Mn<sup>3+</sup> content show an upward tendency. On the contrary, a significant decrease in the Mn<sup>3+</sup> content of NNFM-S was observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0002">2i</a>), replaced by the increased Mn<sup>4+</sup>. This change reveals the successful modulation of electron spin at the Mn site with Sn<sup>4+</sup> input and effectively lessens the adverse effect of Jahn–Teller distortion. The deconvoluted results of Ni 2<i>p</i> and Fe 2<i>p</i> spectrums show no significant difference between NNFM and NNFM-S samples (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting that Ni and Fe sites are insusceptible by Sn<sup>4+</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>Due to the confinement of Jahn–Teller disorder under the effect of electron spin modulation is favorable to sustain the structural integrity of materials. To confirm this, in situ synchrotron high-energy X-ray diffraction (HEXRD) is performed on the NNFM-S and NNFM electrode materials to expose the possible phase transition during battery charge/discharge. According to the observations from 2D contours, the specific diffraction peaks express several variations in shift and intensity (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0003">3a</a>,<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0003">b</a>). With the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, the typical (003) peak indexing O3 phase starts to shift toward a low angle. As the battery charge deepens, this shift consistently turns to the lower values in 2<i>θ</i> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), which implies the gradual expansion of layer spacing stemming from the electrostatic repulsion between O atom layers. Such an expansion behavior highly associates with the depth of Na<sup>+</sup> de-insertion, leading to the formation of P3 phase and its ultimate existence after charging to 4.0&nbsp;V. By comparison, NNFM-S sample shows a stronger signal feature than that of NNFM sample at the whole charge/discharge process. This indicates that the expansion of TM layer spacing is lacking in the counterpart during the charge stage, deriving from the low-degree Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and the weak electrostatic repulsion and thereby promoting the conversion from O3 to O’3 phase instead of P3 phase as-mentioned above. The appearance of O’3 phase can be indexed by the transformed (111) and (11-3) peaks from the original (104) and (018) peaks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). This transformation is relative to the increased Mn<sup>3+</sup> content, then aggravating the Jahn–Teller effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0027_R_d161322420e1213" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Storage Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon further charging, this transient phase can evolute into the P3 phase, and then generate a new heterogeneous phase (OP2) at the end of charging, which can be reflected on the split peak from (012) plane, namely (011) plane at 36.8°.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0028_R_d161322420e1218" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Power Sources Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a>-<a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0031_R_d161322420e1218" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, this alternating octahedral and hexagonal OP2 phase is caused by the sluggish diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> at high voltage. However, the undesirable O’3 and OP2 phases cannot be detected in the NNFM-S sample, showing a better Na<sup>+</sup> de-intercalation rate. Upon the discharging stage, benefiting from the unimpeded Na<sup>+</sup> entrance, the NNFM-S undergoes a facile phase transformation from P3 to O3.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0018_R_d161322420e1230" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>Thee NNFM material suffering from the terrible Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier is the potential to experience multi-level phase conversion and back to the pristine one.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202419967-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c7d2a163-fd86-4131-b6f4-284c046d6be2/adfm202419967-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c7d2a163-fd86-4131-b6f4-284c046d6be2/adfm202419967-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ad7d1902-15fa-4dad-a34a-cea9d2e9a27b/adfm202419967-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c7d2a163-fd86-4131-b6f4-284c046d6be2/adfm202419967-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202419967-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202419967" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Phase transformation and its inhibition mechanism. In situ XRD test and phase transitions for NNFM and NNFM-S. Corresponding 2D contour plots of in situ XRD for a) NNFM-S and b) NNFM samples. Evolution of structure parameters extracted from in situ XRD results, the charge of lattice parameters a for c) NNFM-S and d) NNFM, lattice parameters c for e) NNFM-S and f) NNFM, lattice parameter <i>V</i> for g) NNFM-S and h) NNFM at 0.1 C. i) Image crystal structure evolution during phase transition and transformation paths.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Additionally, the peak values of (101), (012), and (104) shift to a higher one, which demonstrates that the contraction of <i>a</i>-axis and the expansion of <i>c</i>-axis simultaneously arise, as well as volume (<i>V</i>) parameter change. The evolution of these lattice parameters can be tracked by analyzing in situ XRD curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0003">3c–h</a>). In contrast to NNFM sample (3.90%), the unit cell for NNFM-S material shows a lower shrinkage value of 1.91% in <i>a</i>-axis which stands for TM-O bond. The changed percentage in <i>c</i>-axis for NNFM-S also delivers approximately one-third of that in NNFM (1.30% vs 4.08%). Thus, a relatively smaller variation difference in cell volume is acquired in the NNFM-S material. These results reveal the relief of unfavorable Jahn–Teller distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and volume expansion. In a word, the NNFM-S material exhibits the facile phase transformation (O3→ O3+P3→P3→O3) with an inferior volume effect (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0003">3i</a>), while the NNFM material undergoes the complex phase transition of O3→O'3→P3→P3+OP2→P3→O3+P3→O3 and the expansile volume change during charging/discharging. This declares that Sn<sup>4+</sup>-modulated valence at Mn site can effectively relieve the deleterious phase transitions and thereby regulate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>To Probe the kinetic behavior of NNFM-S, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) method, cyclic voltammetry (CV), and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were conducted in the cell with NNFM and NNFM-S cathode. The corresponding calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) was calculated from the GITT curves at the third cycle of battery cycling. The as-obtained <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> value of NNFM-S is between 10<sup>−10</sup>≈10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is largest among the NNFM and others in spite of charging or discharging (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0004">4a,b</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Further, by linear fitting the relation of peak currents and the square root of sweeping rates from the CV curves of the cells with NNFM and NNFM-S cathode at varied scan rates, the higher slope is calculated in the latter as compared with the former (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting the better Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/de-insertion kinetics. Such gooda feature can be further reflected in the Warburg resistance of the cell based on the EIS results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). It is demonstrated that the electron spin state modulation at Mn site with Sn<sup>4+</sup> can reduce the diffusion resistance of Na<sup>+</sup> upon charging/discharging. To understand the detailed role of Sn<sup>4+</sup> in modulating the spin state of Mn<sup>3+</sup> to affect their kinetic behavior, the reasonable 3 × 3 × 1 (Na<sub>27-x</sub>Fe<sub>9</sub>Ni<sub>9</sub>Mn<sub>9</sub>O<sub>53</sub>) supercell with/without Sn substitution was constructed to investigate the (de)intercalation chemistry of Na<sup>+</sup> through density functional theory (DFT) calculations, and the electronic structure and charge density were calculated by optimizing the structure. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0004">4c,a</a> band gap of 0.5&nbsp;eV for Na<sub>27</sub>Fe<sub>9</sub>Ni<sub>9</sub>Mn<sub>9</sub>O<sub>53</sub> supercell (without Sn substitution) is accessed, showing the properties of a semiconductor. In contrast, the electronic distribution of NNFM-S near the Fermi level increases (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0004">4e</a>), leading to the reduced band gap, which is conducive to electron exchange in the material. This demonstrates that Sn<sup>4+</sup>-modulated electron spin effect can be in favor of the electronic conductivity of as-designed materials. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0003">3d,f</a> presents the <i>p</i>DOS of Ni 3<i>d</i> of the two materials. It is found that the band gap of Femi energy level of Ni 3<i>d</i> is 0.54&nbsp;eV for NNFM, while NNFM-S has a value near 0&nbsp;eV in the band gap. By comparison, the better electronic conductivity of Ni<sup>3+</sup> is endowed with NNFM-S materials, in which the involved Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox reactions become more available, thus facilitating the fast proceeding of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/de-intercalation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0004">4d,f</a>). This is the reason why the adverse OP2 phase cannot be detected in the NNFM-S sample mentioned above.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202419967-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5fbf09c8-c6d6-43d3-bc18-f414552851a6/adfm202419967-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5fbf09c8-c6d6-43d3-bc18-f414552851a6/adfm202419967-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/75b0f43a-d3ab-423c-b05f-a2782c1ea513/adfm202419967-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5fbf09c8-c6d6-43d3-bc18-f414552851a6/adfm202419967-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202419967-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202419967" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Kinetic behavior analysis. a) <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> extracted from GITT profiles. b) Variations of the average <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> with the Sn content. The <i>t</i>DOS of c) Na<sub>27</sub>Fe<sub>9</sub>Ni<sub>9</sub>Mn<sub>9</sub>O<sub>53</sub> and d) Na<sub>27</sub>Fe<sub>9</sub>Ni<sub>9</sub>Mn<sub>8</sub>SnO<sub>53</sub>. PDOS of Ni 3<i>s</i> orbital in (d) Na<sub>27</sub>Fe<sub>9</sub>Ni<sub>9</sub>Mn<sub>9</sub>O<sub>53</sub> and (f) Na<sub>27</sub>Fe<sub>9</sub>Ni<sub>9</sub>Mn<sub>8</sub>SnO<sub>53</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>After the elaboration on the valence regulation of Sn<sup>4+</sup>, the as-developed NNFM-S cathode material was expected to enhance the kinetics and Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance. To evaluate this, the electrochemical measurements of the cell with NNFM cathode were operated within the voltage range of 2.0 to 4.0&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). The initial charge and discharge curves of these materials (NNFM, NNFM-S1, NNFM-S, NNFM-S3, NNFM-S4, NNFM-S5) at 0.1 C (1 C = 180 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) has the initial discharge capacities of 132.9, 135.7, 144.8, 107.3, 119.2, 110.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0005">5a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), respectively. Due to the improved kinetics, the rate performance and cycle performance at a high current density of the cell with NNFM-S cathode deserves to be expected. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0005">5b</a>, the cell with NNFM-S cathode has the best rate performance among these contrast samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0005">5b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information), showing the discharge capacity is 164.7, 150.8, 138.7, 116.9, 107.1, 93.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the rates of 0.1 C, 1 C, 2 C, 3 C, 5 C, 10 C, respectively. When the rate returns to 0.1 C, its capacity can quickly recover to 150.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, cyclic voltammetry (CV) was used to examine the reversibility of the cell with NNFM-S cathode, as well as these redox couples, for the first three cycles at a scanning speed of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0005">5c</a>). In the voltage range of 2 – 4&nbsp;V, the curve has two pairs of oxidation/reduction peaks. In the first cycle, the two oxidation peaks at 3.25 and 3.63&nbsp;V belong to Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>and Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup>, respectively. And the reductive ones at 2.78 and 3.61&nbsp;V represent the reduction of Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup>, respectively. Notably, the location and intensity of these redox peaks almost remain constant throughout the cycling, suggesting the high reversibility of the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction behaviors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0032_R_d161322420e1528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the differential capacity versus voltage (<i>dQ/dV</i>) curves at the 1<sup>st</sup>, 20<sup>th</sup>, 50<sup>th</sup>, and 100<sup>th</sup> cycle, derived from the typical voltage profiles, further confirm the good reversibility of NNFM-S material (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). It can be seen that the redox peak ≈2.8&nbsp;V attributes to the phase transition from O3 to P3 shows the gradual-decreased intensity of NNFM electrode with cycling. The oxidation and reduction peaks for NNFM electrodes are 136 and 220&nbsp;mV, respectively. Both of them are higher than that for NNFM-S (121 and 95&nbsp;mV).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202419967-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ae424843-7e69-402b-af16-6313abf7338d/adfm202419967-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ae424843-7e69-402b-af16-6313abf7338d/adfm202419967-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b20c01a0-e46d-41b0-9f32-a0a52f57f527/adfm202419967-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ae424843-7e69-402b-af16-6313abf7338d/adfm202419967-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202419967-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202419967" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance for NNFM and NNFM-S. a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of NNFM and NNFM-S. b) Rate capability and c) CV curves at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. d) cycling performance comparison of NNFM and NNFM-S. e) selected charge/discharge curves. f) Long-term cycling life of NNFM and NNFM-S at 5 C. g) Rate performance comparison of present NNFM-S with previously report O3-type materials.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0005">5d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrate the cyclic performances of the cell with NNFM, NNFM-S1, NNFM-S, NNFM-S3, NNFM-S4 and NNFM-S5 cathodes. It is seen that the capacity retention rates of the cell with NNFM-S are 87.9% and 80.1% in the first 100 cycles and within 200 cycles, respectively. The controlled capacity loss (from 134.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the initial to 108.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the ending) is also harvested. Whereas, the referenced NNFM only provides a low retention of 54.1% after 200 cycles, whose capacity delivers a frequent dropping tendency from the initial 124.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> to the last 70.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The selected charge/discharge curves further reveal the superiority of NNFM-S (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0005">5e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). Benefiting from the accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> kinetic, the cycling performance of NNFM-S material at a high rate of 5 C was also gained (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0005">5f</a>). Within 200 cycles, it can contribute to a high-capacity retention of 78.4% and a good initial capacity of 106.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which are far more than the opponent that contributes to a capacity loss around the 80<sup>th</sup> cycle and the sudden drop of coulombic efficiency (CE) after 160 cycles. The unfavorable features are ascribed to the volume expansion/extraction from Jahn–Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and the accumulation of as-formed OP2 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0033_R_d161322420e1605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results fully demonstrate that the effect of electron spin modulation can confine the volume change from Jahn–Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and sustain the structural stability of TM layers, thus boosting the electrochemical performances for NNFM-S materials. Such an improved layered transition metal oxide cathode can be competitive with the cation-doped O3-Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> cathode material previously reported<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0014_R_d161322420e1617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0023_R_d161322420e1617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Energy Storage Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202419967-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202419967-bib-0034_R_d161322420e1617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Energy Chem.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0005">5g</a>).</p>
         
         <p>To verify the feasibility of NNFM-S material for practical applications, we conducted a full battery test. As demonstrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0006">6a</a>, the battery configuration consists of NNFM-S cathode and a hard carbon anode. In order to eliminate the interference of the anode on the full battery, the hard carbon electrode was first tested in the voltage range of 0.01–2.0&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) to form a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer. This anode has a reversible specific capacity of 260 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). Before assembling the full battery, the activated hard carbon electrode was disassembled and then paired with the NNFM-S electrode. The as-constructed full battery can provide a specific capacity of 126.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and an energy density of 381.0&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the active mass of the cathode) at 0.1 C rate in the voltage window of 1.9 ≈ 4.0&nbsp;V (versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0006">6b</a>). And the specific capacities of 127.0, 116.2, 109.2, 91.2, 75.7 and 45.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> are harvested at 0.1 C, 0.5 C, 1 C,3 C, 5 C and 10 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0006">6c</a>), respectively. This good rate capability can be reflected in the charging/discharging curves at different rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0006">6d</a>). Additionally, the full battery with NNFM-S cathode also has good cycling performance over 200 cycles with a capacity retention as high as 76.8% at 1 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202419967-fig-0006">6e</a>), which shows the great preponderance as compared with these full batteries coupled with hard carbon and layered TM oxide (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Overall, the regulation of the electron spin state at Mn site with high electropositive Sn<sup>4+</sup> can be an effective and promising tactic to develop the layered cathode material for SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202419967-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b31fc9b2-2080-4304-a453-6770b52fd986/adfm202419967-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b31fc9b2-2080-4304-a453-6770b52fd986/adfm202419967-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2935dc3d-688b-4485-85fb-c69808b5dffe/adfm202419967-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b31fc9b2-2080-4304-a453-6770b52fd986/adfm202419967-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202419967-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202419967" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium-ion full batteries. a) Schematic diagram of the full cell employing NNFM-S as the cathode and hard carbon as the anode. b) Initial galvanostatic charge/discharge curve, c) charge/discharge profiles at various rates, d) rate capability, e) cycling performance of the full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202419967-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202419967-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, this work elaborated on the electron spin-state reconfiguration by Sn<sup>4+</sup> incorporation to develop a high-performance NNFM-S material for SIBs. It is found that Sn<sup>4+</sup> cation can able to effectively regulate the MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedral coordination field through Sn-O-Mn interactions, leading to the transformation of Mn from high-spin state (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/72740d23-5ce6-41dd-9147-d7bba5b20761/adfm202419967-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202419967:adfm202419967-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202419967-math-0003.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>t</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <mi>g</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>3</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>e</mi>
                        <mi>g</mi>
                        <mn>1</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$t_{2g}^3e_g^1$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) to the low-spin state (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/57446379-903a-4482-9229-295bbbaac965/adfm202419967-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202419967:adfm202419967-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202419967-math-0004.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>t</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <mi>g</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>4</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>e</mi>
                        <mi>g</mi>
                        <mn>0</mn>
                     </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$t_{2g}^4e_g^0$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>), as revealed by the density of states and valence analysis. Such an alteration effectively mitigates the Jahn–Teller distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> inside materials and then inhibits deleterious phase transitions and volume expansion, realizing the fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics upon the charging/discharging process. Consequently, the cell/full battery with NNFM-S cathode renders significantly improved cycling and rate performances. We anticipate that this work will provide the in-depth insights into the design of layered transition metal oxide for use in SIBs, suggesting the pivotal role of electron spin at TM sites and their modulation as a promising strategy for advanced battery development.</p>
         </section>
